The last and third part in the series will focus on psychological processes, such as memory and learning, and what we can learn from that with respect to deep learning.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="485"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;h4&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;strong&gt;</span>Acknowledgments<span class="html-tag">&lt;/strong&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;/h4&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="486"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>I would like to thank Alexander Tonn for his useful advice and for proofreading this blog post.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="487"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;h4&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;strong&gt;</span>Important references and sources&nbsp;<span class="html-tag">&lt;/strong&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;/h4&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="488"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;strong&gt;</span>Neuroscience<span class="html-tag">&lt;/strong&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="489"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Brunel, N., Hakim, V., &amp;amp; Richardson, M. class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-meta --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="553"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="554"></td><td class="line-content">				<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-content</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="555"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Very interesting and thorough. I&amp;#8217;m just a programmer so not properly qualified to respond but I had to say that your argument that the brain is well optimised doesn&amp;#8217;t seem to hold water.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="556"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>You reason that because there is resource pressure for brains to be small that means they must be fully optimised. That&amp;#8217;s like building a program in a slow running language like javascript, provisioning the minimum possible machine size for it then concluding that it couldn&amp;#8217;t be much smaller if written in C++. In fact, I wouldn&amp;#8217;t be surprised if we discover the brain to be millions of times less efficient than our inventions in terms of excess system complexity. Energy consumption may turn out to be another matter though. <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="557"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Evolution doesn&amp;#8217;t necessarily move forward all the time or strategise its designs well. It has the benefit of time to look clever but in many ways it isn&amp;#8217;t.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="558"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Before planes, many thought that we would need a system with the complexity and fluidity of flapping wing to achieve agile flight. By coming to understand the underlying principles we achieved nearly the same goals with much simpler technology.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="559"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Of course, intelligence is a far harder problem than flight and it may be that the first effective AI will not be mostly human designed but need to build itself through an evolution-like process such that nobody understands. But I tend to think we&amp;#8217;ll be able to step in at a few points and make some decisive tweaks.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="560"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>I just don&amp;#8217;t buy the defence of the brain&amp;#8217;s magnificence. If the brain were well optimised for useful work:<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="561"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>1) It wouldn&amp;#8217;t make the kind of constant mistakes in reasoning which mean that smart people can be hugely more effective than average ones. If the retort to this is that effectiveness != evolutionary fitness then that wins the argument against brains too.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="562"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>2) We wouldn&amp;#8217;t need to have fine-grained discussions about why current AI visual systems aren&amp;#8217;t really better than human&amp;#8217;s even though they could appear to be. You show that our brain is at least a million times bigger than a supercomputer so if it were optimised, this wouldn&amp;#8217;t be debatable. We would have nothing that slightly resembles AI yet.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="563"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>3) The cerebellum wouldn&amp;#8217;t require that 3 quarters computational power just for orchestrating muscle movements considering we have robots which do it on a simpler level already.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="564"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>4) It wouldn&amp;#8217;t keep producing smart people who are mentally ill.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="565"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Is there something big I&amp;#8217;m misunderstanding?<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="566"></td><td class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-metadata</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="578"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;a <span class="html-attribute-name">href</span>="<a class="html-attribute-value html-external-link" target="_blank" href="https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-430">https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-430</a>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="579"></td><td class="line-content">							<span class="html-tag">&lt;time <span class="html-attribute-name">datetime</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">2015-07-27T16:25:50+00:00</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="580"></td><td class="line-content">								July 27, 2015 at 4:25 pm							<span class="html-tag">&lt;/time&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="581"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="582"></td><td class="line-content">											<span class="html-tag">&lt;/div&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-metadata --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="583"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="584"></td><td class="line-content">									<span class="html-tag">&lt;/footer&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-meta --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="585"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="586"></td><td class="line-content">				<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-content</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="587"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>1) Google trained a convolutional net on many hundreds of million of images and I believe it had an accuracy of about 20% (80% error); if you increase the possible knowledge and decision making, you increase the possibility for error exponentially. This is why ImageNet is so much harder than other data sets, and this is the same reason we as humans do mistakes on a daily basis. It is the same as: The more you know, the more you don&amp;#8217;t know.<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="588"></td><td class="line-content">2) Arguably, we have nothing that resembles AI yet; our deep learning systems are currently able to recognize objects in images which is a basic, non-cognitive (in the sense of &amp;#8220;thinking&amp;#8221;) ability of the brain. It is true that we have systems which throw sentence creation from images in the mix, but arguably these systems do not have a real understanding what language is<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="589"></td><td class="line-content">3) We do not have robots with the same degrees of freedom that animals have: Try to get a robot to throw a ball in the same way as humans do — robots currently are not able to do this<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="590"></td><td class="line-content">4) Current views seem to suggest that genes that have a probability to cause a certain mental illness are good as long as you do not have the disease. Or in other words: Genes that make you schizophrenic, depressed, psychopathic, or psychotic increases your fitness (creativity, intelligence, sociability) as long as you do not have schizophrenia, depression, psychopathy or delusions in the first place. Of course this is much more complex, for example in shamanistic societies schizophrenia may increase your fitness dramatically because you easily become the religious authority due to your ability to receive &amp;#8220;prophecies&amp;#8221;; this may be another reason why we will have genes that cause schizophrenia — remember that most humans may have lived in such societies at one point. <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="591"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Thus I do not think it is correct to say that nature does something wrong by producing mental illness. If you model gene inheritance distributions with a markov chain that will settle to probability distributions which produce optimal fitness according to rules governed by evolutionary game theory, you will probably see that genes that cause mental illness are necessary to maximize the fitness of an overall gene pool (there are certainly papers on this which you can look up) — thus mental illness is inevitable under optimal conditions.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="592"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Does this make more sense now?<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="593"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p <span class="html-attribute-name">id</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-like-430</span>" <span class="html-attribute-name">data-liked</span>=<span class="line-number" value="731"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>I used to work at Numenta (as a side note, all those &amp;#8220;dreams&amp;#8221; generated top-down by DBNs of one kind or another are something we used to do&amp;#8230; 5 years ago, or at least we observed it, but didn&amp;#8217;t pursue). I would say the #1 problem we had was scale, and there was no technology in existence to approach the scale of the brain. The second problem, IMHO, is that it is not yet entirely clear how the brain does what it does exactly. We have many pieces of the puzzle, but maybe not a complete picture yet (I haven&amp;#8217;t checked the neuroscience literature lately though). I don&amp;#8217;t know if it is true that brains would be just a souped up DBN, for example, although it seems that deserves to be explored. But some people will say that QM is involved in brain processing &amp;#8211; I have no opinion myself as I am not a neuroscientist, but I&amp;#8217;ve heard it. All in all, it seemed to me that at the time (5 years ago), there were still a lot of unknowns about the brain.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-author --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="810"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="811"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-metadata</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="812"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;a <span class="html-attribute-name">href</span>="<a class="html-attribute-value html-external-link" target="_blank" href="https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-444">https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-444</a>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="813"></td><td class="line-content">							<span class="html-tag">&lt;time <span class="html-attribute-name">datetime</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">2015-07-28T07:43:38+00:00</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="814"></td><td class="line-content">								July 28, 2015 at 7:43 am							<span class="html-tag">&lt;/time&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="815"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="816"></td><td class="line-content">											<span class="html-tag">&lt;/div&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-metadata --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="817"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="818"></td><td class="line-content">									<span class="html-tag">&lt;/footer&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-meta --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="819"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="820"></td><td class="line-content">				<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-content</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="821"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>The part comparing a single neuron to an ANN is very interesting, however I&amp;#8217;m not sure about the later part where the flops between brain and high performance super computer are compared directly. It seems to me the brain and computer are using completely different optimization strategies. It is true that brain has a huge number of  nodes that can do information processing, and it uses very little power, but at least from my limited understanding, it is also very inaccurate due to its analog nature, i.e. if you give a neuron the same input you don&amp;#8217;t always get the exact same output, this is why you have some random terms in the simulation. Computers on the other hand are very reliable, you always get the same output given the same input, we want it this way and it has a high price in terms of complexity and power usage (for example we need a relatively big voltage difference to differentiate 0 from 1, because we really don&amp;#8217;t want to mistaken 1 for 0).<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="822"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>So a direct emulation of brain using computer is using the weakness of computer (high power, low parallelism) to emulate the strength of brain (lower power, high parallelism), the result can&amp;#8217;t be good. In order to get better results, you either need to play to computer&amp;#8217;s strength, which I think would mean better algorithm which rely on the accuracy of computer; or you redesign the machine to be more like neurons. The former is already discussed in the blog post, and I like Yann LeCun&amp;#8217;s argument. For the latter, there&amp;#8217;re some non-Von Neumann architectures such as memcomputing, or neuromorphic chip that may show promise. If you compare the size of a transistor (14nm in production, 5nm in 5 years) to the size of a neuron (4000nm diameter for the smallest one), we can pack a whole lot of transistors in the footprint of a neuron, the question is how do we make use of these transistors.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="823"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p <span class="html-attribute-name">id</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-like-444</span>" <span class="html-attribute-name">data-liked</span>=<span class="line-number" value="844"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>These are all good arguments that you give here. With this blog post I tried to establish something which is practically useful and because analog computers are not used widely or successfully you cannot avoid the awkwardness of transforming analog computation to digital one. I think my approach of mathematical modelling and then uses the known complexities for the mathematics on digital computers is the fairest comparison that we can do as of now.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="845"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>The problem with memristors, and non-Von-Neumann architectures is that no successful computers exists which make use of these ideas in a practical sense, and thus it is hard to include them into a practical framework — there is just no data I can base any estimates on. I will surely revise my estimates once such computer become available for practical computational tasks.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" class="line-number" value="942"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>As I pointed out above, currently we have nothing better than use make estimates that make sense for the comparison with digital computers.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="943"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Neuromorphic chips as they are designed now will unlikely be used for deep learning in the future. Currently, the IBM chip only reaches 15% error on MNIST, which is worse than linear methods. This also demonstrates that simple models of the brain are unlikely to work (such chips often use a version of the integrate-and-fire neurons, which are also featured for the estimate of Ray Kurzweil). <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="944"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Also see <span class="line-number" value="968"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Witali Dunin-Barkowski 6:13 PM:<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="969"></td><td class="line-content">Your table in section &amp;#8220;Estimation of cerebellar input/output dimensions&amp;#8221; yields wonderful 10^11 for the nuber of neurons (correct, this is the number of granule cells) and 10^21 for the number of operations/sec. This gives 10^10 ops/sec for the granule cell. Indeed, the granule cell sometimes can fire with the freqyency up to 500 imp/s, but very rare. The exact number is not known, but the average frequency of granule cells firing is in the region of 10 imp/s. With 5 incoming and up to five hundreds outcoming connections these numbers in no case will result in<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="970"></td><td class="line-content">10^10 ops/sec. I would evaluatre that in about (505 synapses)x(10 imp/s) ~ &amp;lt;10^4 ops/sec.  This is much more realistic, and can be attained in artificial systems.﻿<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="971"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Witali Dunin-Barkowski 7:40 PM+1:<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="972"></td><td class="line-content">&amp;quot;An average neuron in the cerebellum has about 25000 million synapses&amp;quot; &amp;#8211; are you sure (misprint?)? If you count granule cells, they have ~500 synapses (almost all &amp;#8211; outcoming). Purkinje cells might have up to 200 000 synapses (much more incoming than outcoming).﻿<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="973"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Witali Dunin-Barkowski 8:22 PM+1:<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="974"></td><td class="line-content">&amp;quot;The neurotransmitter (2) binds to a G-protein which then produces a protein&amp;quot; &amp;#8211; alas! You are not right.<span class="html-tag">&lt;br /&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="975"></td><td class="line-content">In fact, almost all synapses have ionotropic and metabotropic receptors at the postsynaptic side. Transmitters connect with approximtely equal chances with both types of receptors. Activation of ionotropic receptors leads to electrical excitation/inhibition interactions. Activation of metabotropic receptors starts signaling cascades processes. Both metabotropic and ionotropic processes flow in parallel, although the latter are much slower than the former. Because of these inaccuracies of handling the facts of experimental neurosciences I would not advise your readers to take seriously your conclusions regarding possibility of impossibility of the singularity.﻿<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="976"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p <span class="line-number" value="1012"></td><td class="line-content">							<span class="html-tag">&lt;time <span class="html-attribute-name">datetime</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">2015-07-29T06:24:04+00:00</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1013"></td><td class="line-content">								July 29, 2015 at 6:24 am							<span class="html-tag">&lt;/time&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1014"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1015"></td><td class="line-content">											<span class="html-tag">&lt;/div&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-metadata --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1016"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1017"></td><td class="line-content">									<span class="html-tag">&lt;/footer&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-meta --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1018"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1019"></td><td class="line-content">				<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-content</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1020"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>I will write more about the bird and aircraft argument in an update. <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1021"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>As for electrons in semiconductors: They do not contain any information because their flow is just random (this can be modelled as a random walk). The flow of particles in dendrites is not just a random walk, but a random walk along a concentration gradient which is maintained in distinct patterns by the cell (this can be modelled by either convolution or equivalently partial differential equations). <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1022"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>You do not need partial differential equation to model the flow of electrons in semiconductors to make predictions about its flow. A model build on variables for the electrical conductivity of the material and time will be an excellent model for the flow of electrons in a semiconductor.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1023"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Thus, if I take into account the diffusion of electrons in semiconductors, it will add zero computation; however, it was shown that dendrites are not passive cables like, but do meaningful computation that contributes significantly to information processing. There are dozen of papers about this now; but just have a look at the Nature paper in my reference list if you have any doubt about this.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="html-attribute-value">says</span>"&gt;</span>says:<span class="html-tag">&lt;/span&gt;</span>					<span class="html-tag">&lt;/div&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-author class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-metadata</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1038"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;a <span class="html-attribute-name">href</span>="<a class="html-attribute-value html-external-link" target="_blank" href="https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-453">https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/comment-page-1/#comment-453</a>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1039"></td><td class="line-content">							<span class="html-tag">&lt;time <span class="html-attribute-name">datetime</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">2015-07-29T01:04:00+00:00</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1040"></td><td class="line-content">								July 29, 2015 at 1:04 am							<span class="html-tag">&lt;/time&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1041"></td><td class="line-content">						<span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1042"></td><td class="line-content">											<span class="html-tag">&lt;/div&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-metadata --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1043"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1044"></td><td class="line-content">									<span class="html-tag">&lt;/footer&gt;</span><span class="html-comment">&lt;!-- .comment-meta --&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1045"></td><td class="line-content"><br></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1046"></td><td class="line-content">				<span class="html-tag">&lt;div <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-content</span>"&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1047"></td><td class="line-content">					<span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>This is one of the best descriptions of the computational problems of intelligence and brain modeling I have ever seen.  I work on machine consciousness and fret about these very things. The computational requirements for human level cognition do look enormous.  But, I have three critiques of your conclusions&amp;#8230; well, more hopes than critiques actually.   <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1048"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>1) Brain size may be largely irrelevant to intelligence and consciousness.  eg there are examples of high functioning individuals with hydrocephalus who have much smaller than normal sized brains: <span class="html-tag">&lt;a <span class="html-attribute-name">href</span>="<a class="html-attribute-value html-external-link" target="_blank" href="https://www.newscientist.com/article/dn12301-man-with-tiny-brain-shocks-doctors/">https://www.newscientist.com/article/dn12301-man-with-tiny-brain-shocks-doctors/</a>" <span class="html-attribute-name">rel</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">nofollow</span>"&gt;</span>https://www.newscientist.com/article/dn12301-man-with-tiny-brain-shocks-doctors/<span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span>   The brain size necessary to achieve general intelligence may be substantially smaller than we think.  An elephant&amp;#8217;s brain is over 3 times larger (both in volume and neurons), but we don&amp;#8217;t think of elephants as being more intelligent than humans.  Bird brains are much smaller and different than human brains and birds engage in a whole host of useful, intelligent behavior as demonstrated by parrots and crows, including memory, pattern and object recognition, performance of complex tasks, and even some language.   Hopefully, brain size, based on number of neurons, poorly correlates to intelligence. <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1049"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>2) How much of the brain is actually dedicated to what we could call the representational problems? It could be that we are not that intelligent at all, in terms of mass to cognitive power. And there is good evidence for this. The child prodigy has no more brain mass than an ordinary child but a bit more than a child with down-syndrome.  But we know it&amp;#8217;s not the size of the brain of the down-syndrome child that seems to matter, it&amp;#8217;s how the cells themselves are structured and behave. This is almost certainly true for the child prodigy.  What matters about brains isn&amp;#8217;t volume per se, but cell structure and function (you cover this very well btw). If we understood how the structure and interaction of neurons (and other brain cells) produces cognition, we may discover that intelligence could be achieved a whole lot more efficiently than how it&amp;#8217;s currently done in brains.  <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1050"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>3) Even though we are approaching silicon and lithographic limits of processor manufacture, we are not necessarily constrained to those approaches. The brain is a bunch of molecules, proving there is no theoretical reason we could not construct other kinds of molecular machines that reproduce the cognitive functions of the brain that do not rely on our existing processor technologies.  Other approaches may let us get further down the road of Moore&amp;#8217;s law without the costs you suggest.    <span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1051"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p&gt;</span>Otherwise, I think you&amp;#8217;re analysis is correct&amp;#8230;. I just hope it&amp;#8217;s wrong.<span class="html-tag">&lt;/p&gt;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="line-number" value="1052"></td><td class="line-content"><span class="html-tag">&lt;p <span class="html-attribute-name">id</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-like-453</span>" <span class="html-attribute-name">data-liked</span>=<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-not-liked</span> <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-likes comment-not-liked</span>"&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;a <span class="html-attribute-name">href</span>="<a class="html-attribute-value html-external-link" target="_blank" href="https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/?like_comment=453&_wpnonce=b1b9f598c7">https://timdettmers.wordpress.com/2015/07/27/brain-vs-deep-learning-singularity/?like_comment=453&amp;#038;_wpnonce=b1b9f598c7</a>" <span class="html-attribute-name">class</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">comment-like-link needs-login</span>" <span class="html-attribute-name">rel</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">nofollow</span>" <span class="html-attribute-name">data-blog</span>="<span class="html-attribute-value">72358744</span>"&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;span&gt;</span>Like<span class="html-tag">&lt;/span&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;/a&gt;</span><span class="html-tag">&lt;span <span
